        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116"><meta property="og:title" content="Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl4 Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.9b00116.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Titanium tetrachloride is reacted with hydroxide groups on cellulose (cotton wool) before firing to convert the cellulose to hard carbon. Hard carbon-nanocrystalline titanium nitride composites with a good distribution of the titanium across the fibrous hard carbon structure were obtained by firing the treated cellulose under nitrogen. Hard carbon-nanocrystalline titanium carbide composites were obtained by firing under argon. The composites were tested as anode materials for sodium ion batteries, and the HC-TiN composite delivers a better capacity retention than that of hard carbon over 50 cycles. The synthesis method demonstrated here provides an effective route to composites of metal nitrides and carbides with carbon that may be of interest for other energy technologies as well as for sodium batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.9b00116.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187da4bee4075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187da4bee4075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'cd46954f-3bce-4688-a74b-cc162391a6d7'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" async="" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187da4bee4075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'inorganic_chemistry';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i <script nonce="93187da4bee4075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "inocaj";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

            </li>
            <li>
                <a title="Cited By" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#citeThis" data-id="article_header_JumpTo_Cited%20By">
                   <span>Cited By</span>
                </a>
            </li></ul></div></li><li class="share__item share__item--close"><button data-id="article_header_Expand" class="share__ctrl share__ctrl--article"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-arrows-expand"></i><span class="expand">Expand</span><span class="compress">Collapse</span></button></li></ul></div>

        </div></div><div class="article__pubdate"><span>Article</span><time datetime="2019-04-25">April 25, 2019</time></div><h1 class="article_header-title" tabindex="0"><span class="hlFld-Title" property="headline">Synthesis of Hard Carbon-TiN/TiC Composites by Reacting Cellulose with TiCl<sub>4</sub> Followed by Carbothermal Nitridation/Reduction</span><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy article link</span><span Soulé</a></span></div></div></div></span></li></ul><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Titanium tetrachloride is reacted with hydroxide groups on cellulose (cotton wool) before firing to convert the cellulose to hard carbon. Hard carbon-nanocrystalline titanium nitride composites with a good distribution of the titanium across the fibrous hard carbon structure were obtained by firing the treated cellulose under nitrogen. Hard carbon-nanocrystalline titanium carbide composites were obtained by firing under argon. The composites were tested as anode materials for sodium ion batteries, and the HC-TiN composite delivers a better capacity retention than that of hard carbon over 50 cycles. The synthesis method demonstrated here provides an effective route to composites of metal nitrides and carbides with carbon that may be of interest for other energy technologies as well as for sodium batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5776%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHang%2BCheng%252C%2BNuria%2BGarcia-Araez%252C%2BAndrew%2BL.%2BHector%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D58%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacs.inorgchem.9b00116%26title%3DSynthesis%2Bof%2BHard%2BCarbon-TiN%252FTiC%2BComposites%2Bby%2BReacting%2BCellulose%2Bwith%2BTiCl4%2BFollowed%2Bby%2BCarbothermal%2BNitridation%252FReduction%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0020-1669%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dinocaj%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5786%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292348&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Cellulose"> Cellulose </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292111&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrides"> Nitrides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291571&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Titanium"> Titanium </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="hlFld-Abstract synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><span><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Titanium tetrachloride is reacted with hydroxide groups on cellulose (cotton wool) before firing to convert the cellulose to hard carbon. The synthesis method demonstrated here provides an effective route to composites of metal nitrides and carbides with carbon that may be of interest for other energy technologies as well as for sodium batteries.</p></span></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Transition metal nitrides/carbides are promising electrode materials in energy applications due to a combination of high electronic conductivity <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> with electrocatalytic activity or charge storage capabilities, with the latter sometimes due to surface oxide layers. MoN and WC exhibit high Pt-like electrocatalytic activities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> VN and TiC have been successfully applied in supercapacitors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> A series of transition metal nitrides (Cu<sub>3</sub>N, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Co<sub>3</sub>N, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and Fe<sub>3</sub>N) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and a transition metal carbide (Mo<sub>2</sub>C) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> have been explored as anode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and recently a number of metal nitrides (MN<sub><i>x</i></sub>, M = Cu, Mn, Ni, Sn, etc.) synthesized by our group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> and the VN reported by Cui et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> have shown promising electrochemical properties for sodium ion batteries. In several of these systems, there is evidence that conversion reactions (reduction to the metal and sodium nitride) occur mainly at particle surfaces; hence, reducing particle size will be important in maximizing their capacities. Zhang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> reported Mo<sub>2</sub>C as a sodium storage material with good cycling and rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Carbon-based composites in electrochemical applications provide good electrical conductivity and high chemical resistance. Carbon is routinely used as a conductivity additive in battery and supercapacitor electrode fabrication. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Carbon supported platinum metals and oxides in fuel cells preserve nanoparticle surface area while providing good conduction pathways. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Recently, extensive work on supporting a range of materials on carbon to exploit these properties has been reported. For example, mesoporous TiN/C microfibers are more efficient than Pt/C for both the oxygen reduction and the oxygen evolution reaction in Li–O<sub>2</sub> cells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Yousef et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> embedded Co-TiC nanoparticles on carbon nanofibers and then used the composite for fuel cells and dye-sensitized solar cells. Composites for battery applications include Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on graphene with a reversible capacity of 935 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> vs Li, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and Mo<sub>2</sub>C nanoparticles encapsulated into a porous N-doped carbon matrix that achieved a capacity retention of 95.4% after 2500 cycles vs Na. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">A number of strategies have been used to synthesize carbon-supported metal nitrides and carbides. For instance, Zhu et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> synthesized MoN@nitrogen-doped carbon electrocatalysts by decomposing a metal–organic framework to MoO<sub>2</sub>–C followed by calcination under ammonia. Zhang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> deposited titanium nitride on carbon fibers using a sol–gel method and then applied this coating in flexible supercapacitors. Cao et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> applied a solvothermal method followed by firing at 550 °C under an H<sub>2</sub>–Ar mix to obtain Mo<sub>2</sub>C–C hybrid nanospheres, displaying a better cyclic performance in LIBs than bulk Mo<sub>2</sub>C.</div><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion battery (SIB) research started with that on LIBs in the 1980s, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> but while LIBs have achieved massive commercial success, effort into SIBs was minimal for the following two decades. There are potential future problems with the growing cost of lithium as usage of LIBs in transport and large-scale storage is increasing. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The low cost and abundant supply of sodium <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> have driven a significant recent upturn in SIB interest in the past few years, with 4 papers published in 2010 and 1464 in 2018 (Web of Science Core Collection, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> March 25, 2019 with topic of “sodium ion batter*”). The best-known anodic host material in LIBs, graphite, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> does not intercalate sodium to any significant degree due to an ionic radius 55% larger than that of lithium. Hard carbon (HC) is an attractive alternative to graphite for SIBs, with a good conductivity and fairly high reversible capacity at low voltage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> HC is obtained by pyrolysis of various precursors including resorcinol-formaldehyde, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> glucose <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> and peat moss. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Cotton wool is a promising low cost, renewable precursor for synthesis of hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Our interest is in producing composite materials with hard carbon to improve its capacity and capacity retention with cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we report an effective method to obtain HC-TiN or HC-TiC composites by reacting a reactive metal precursor with cellulose before firing to convert the cellulose to HC. Carbothermal nitridation under N<sub>2</sub> gas yielded HC-TiN, whereas carbothermal reduction under argon produced HC-TiC. Both composites show similar first cycle capacities to HC, but the titanium nitride composite delivers a better capacity retention (85.2%) after 50 cycles than that of hard carbon (74.3%). We expect the synthesis method demonstrated here to provide an effective route to composites with other metal nitrides and carbides for sodium cell and other energy technologies.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The composites were synthesized by reacting cotton wool with TiCl<sub>4</sub> then firing in nitrogen or argon. Cotton wool (5 g, Fisher Scientific) was dried in vacuo overnight. It was then placed into a flask under N<sub>2</sub> and covered with hexane (200 mL, Fisher Scientific, distilled from sodium/benzophenone ketyl ether) followed by addition of different volumes of TiCl<sub>4</sub> (between 0 and 0.5 mL, Sigma-Aldrich, 99.9% trace metals basis). The mixture was heated to reflux overnight, and then the solvent and any remaining precursor was removed in vacuo. The dried material was transferred into a furnace tube in the glovebox and then fired under nitrogen or argon. Firing was carried out with a temperature ramp of 4 °C min<sup>–1</sup> to 1400 °C and this temperature was maintained for 2 h. Pure hard carbon (HC) samples were synthesized for comparison using the same steps but without adding the TiCl<sub>4</sub> during synthesis.</div><div class="NLM_p">Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were collected in 0.7 mm silica capillaries with parallel Cu Kα X-rays using a Rigaku Smartlab. XRD data analysis used the Rigaku PDXL2 and GSAS packages. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Raman spectra were collected with a Renishaw inVia Ramascope operating at 785 nm with an energy of 7.08 mW. Raman peak analysis used WiRE software, a linear baseline correction and curve-fitting of the three peaks (G, D, and D3) using Gaussian and Lorentzian functions. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was measured with a TG 209 F1 Libra with a ramp rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> with flowing gas mixture of Ar (50 mL min<sup>–1</sup>) and O<sub>2</sub> (20 mL min<sup>–1</sup>). Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) used a Philips XL30 and a Jeol JSM6500 with 10 kV accelerating voltage. Energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) analysis used a Thermofisher Ultradry detector. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) used a FEI Tecnai T12 (80 kV). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried on a Thermo Nexsa with Al Kα X-rays. XPS data analysis was conducted from the Casa XPS software package. The XPS binding energy scale was calibrated to graphitic carbon at 284.6 eV. Core peaks were fitted with nonlinear Shirley-type background. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Peak positions and areas were optimized by a weighted least-squares fitting method using 70% Gaussian and 30% Lorentzian line shapes. The surface areas were calculated by the Brunauer–Emmet–Teller (BET) method from nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms obtained on a Micromeritics Tristar II surface area analyzer.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Working electrodes were prepared from inks produced using 0.2 g of composite or HC with a polyvinylidene difluoride (Solvay) binder at a weight ratio of 95:5 wt %. These materials were mixed with <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (0.5–0.6 mL, anhydrous, 99.5%, Sigma-Aldrich) to prepare a viscous ink, which was mixed with an homogenizer (running at speeds of 10 000, 15 000, and 20 000 rpm for 5, 3, and 2 min, respectively) and cast onto copper foil (0.0175 mm thick, Goodfellow Cambridge Ltd.) using a K-bar of 200 μm. The ink was air-dried before the foil was cut into 11 mm diameter discs, and then further dried under vacuum overnight. Typical mass loadings of electrodes prepared in this way were 2–2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Discs of sodium with diameter of 11 mm (Sigma-Aldrich) were used as the counter and reference electrode. Two sheets of dried Whatman GF/D glass fiber (GE Healthcare Life Sciences) were used as the separator, soaked with 180 μL of the electrolyte which was made up from 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Alfa Aesar, anhydrous) in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC, Sigma-Aldrich, anhydrous, 99%) and diethyl carbonate (DEC, Sigma-Aldrich, anhydrous, ≥99%) in a 1:1 ratio in volume. All the electrochemical tests were conducted using Swagelok cells and all the cell assembly was carried out in an argon-filled glovebox (Mbraun, H<sub>2</sub>0 &lt; 0.1 ppm, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1 ppm). Galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling was performed at 25 °C in the voltage range of 5 mV to 2 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) using a Biologic MPG potentiostat. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was obtained in the frequency range from 0.05 Hz to 500 kHz at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a> illustrates the synthesis process of the HC-TiN and HC-TiC composites. The main component in cotton wool is cellulose (∼95%), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> which is a polymer of β-<span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">d</span>-glucose. The hydroxide groups on the cellulose are expected to react with the TiCl<sub>4</sub> to form C–O–Ti bonds with HCl as the byproduct. Initially reactions were carried out at room temperature since the TiCl<sub>4</sub> was expected to be highly reactive with these hydroxide groups, but it was found that titanium loadings on the cellulose were higher when the solution was refluxed. This may be related to poor wetting of the polar cellulose surfaces by the low polarity hexane solvent. Different volumes of TiCl<sub>4</sub> (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) were added to obtain composites with various Ti loadings. After firing, composites of TiN or TiC with hard carbon were produced. The amount of the titanium-containing phase in these composites was measured by heating the samples in the TGA (Supporting Information, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) to remove the carbon and oxidize the titanium to TiO<sub>2</sub>.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0012.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Synthesis Process Used to Make HC-TiN and HC-TiC Composites</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. char=".">41</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t1fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t1fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Hard carbon obtained under nitrogen gas.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn2"><sup><sup>b</sup></sup><p class="last">Hard carbon obtained under argon gas.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The fibrous microstructure of the cellulose is obvious in the SEM images of the HC, even after grinding the samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>). Samples that had been reacted with TiCl<sub>4</sub> before pyrolysis had an obvious surface roughening, increasing with the titanium loading and with nanoparticles visible on the surfaces of the fibrous structures, and TEM images confirmed this observation as well as showing crystallite sizes consistent with those listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2–S4</a>). The images suggest that the surfaces are quite consistent within a particular sample, and the corresponding EDX mapping images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>) reveal that the distribution of titanium across the HC structure is very uniform.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of HC-TiN (a and b) and HC-TiC (d and e) composites. The purple dots in the EDX maps (c and f) represent the dispersion of titanium in these samples and are acquired in the same regions as the corresponding SEM images (b and e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM images of HC-TiN (a) and HC-TiC (b) composites showing crystallites at the surfaces and hard carbon domain features in the body of the fibers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> displays the XRD patterns of composites with different mass loadings of titanium nitride or carbide. The broad peaks around 23° correspond to the 002 peak of the graphitic domains of the hard carbon <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> (data refinement can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). This peak can be used to calculate the interlayer <i>d</i>-spacing of the hard carbon (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), which was found to increase with titanium loading. A wider interlayer separation has been linked to better sodium ion insertion and deinsertion <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> and may suggest some limited titanium insertion between the layers. The interlayer spacing of thermally expanded graphite can reach 0.43 nm, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> and others have observed increased interlayer spacings on introducing aluminum ions into graphite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The increases in interlayer spacing observed here are plausible. A second broad HC feature is also observed around 44°, corresponding to the 100 peak of the graphitic domains.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XRD patterns of HC-TiN (a) and HC-TiC (b) composites, with loadings as shown.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As the titanium loading increased, the sharper peaks increased in intensity. These had the familiar rock-salt-type pattern expected for the cubic TiC, TiN, or TiO. The patterns were refined and the extracted lattice parameters are shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, the lattice parameters are compared with those reported in the Inorganic Crystal Structure Database (ICSD) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> for the TiC, TiN, or TiO phases that were considered to be potential constituents of the composites. It can be seen that the lattice parameter decreases fairly linearly from TiC (∼4.32 Å) to TiN (∼4.24 Å) and TiO (∼4.17 Å) and that the reported lattice parameters for these phases occur in quite narrow bands. The lattice parameters from <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> are shown as two bands in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, where it is clear that the composites fired in nitrogen all have lattice parameters close to the ICSD values for TiN, and those fired in argon are all close to TiC. These systems are known to form solid solutions, and the presence of the other two anions in the samples cannot be ruled out, but this is difficult to measure in the presence of a large excess of carbon. For both composites, the diffraction peaks become narrower as the titanium loading increases, implying that the crystallite sizes become larger. The HC-TiN composites have broader diffraction peaks than the HC-TiC composites with similar TiCl<sub>4</sub> concentrations. Crystallite size of TiN varies from 3.6 to 34.0 nm, while that of TiC varies from 19.1 to 38.0 nm (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, data fitted in the PDXL package).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Symbols and best-fit line showing the literature lattice parameters of TiC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 48 to 52">(48−52)</a> TiN, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 49 and 53 to 57">(49,53−57)</a> and TiO <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 58 to 62">(58−62)</a> from ICSD. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The blue and gray banded regions represent the range of lattice parameters found for the components designated as TiN or TiC (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) in the composites obtained in this study.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The melting points of titanium nitride and titanium carbide are 3228 K (2930 °C) and 3458 K (3160 °C), respectively. Tammann temperatures are typically 50–75% of the melting point and are expected to be above 1614 and 1729 K, respectively. The synthesis temperature in our experiment is 1698 K (1400 °C). Hence, the larger titanium carbide crystallite sizes are not consistent with annealing rates at this firing temperature. However, the synthesis temperature is 86% of the melting point of titanium (1966 K or 1668 °C), which is a potential intermediate during the synthesis process. After reaction with the oxidic cellulose surface, the titanium is likely to be present in a form chemically similar to TiO<sub>2</sub>. The high temperature synthesis is likely to involve carbothermal reduction of the titanium-containing species either combined with (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>) or followed by (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eqs 3">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>) reaction with gaseous nitrogen or further carbon from the HC surface. The larger crystallite sizes of TiC suggest that <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eqs 3">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a> are important since if the more sinterable titanium metal is present during the synthesis then it could react more rapidly to make TiN utilizing gas phase diffusion of nitrogen than to make TiC by solid-state diffusion of carbon.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectroscopy is particularly useful to investigate the HC component of the composites when the PXRD is dominated by the metal nitrides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, the Raman plots show broad peaks due to hard carbon around 1310 and 1595 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which can be attributed to the disordered (D-band) and ordered graphitic (G-band) structures in hard carbon, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref64" aria-label="reference 43 and 64">(43,64)</a> The spectral analysis by curve fitting is illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The D and G bands were fitted with Lorentzian functions and a small D3 band contribution (amorphous carbon, 1500 cm<sup>–1</sup>) as a Gaussian function as described by others. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> The integrated intensity of the D-band is stronger than that of G-band, suggesting that the composites retain the highly disordered graphitic structure of hard carbon. The integrated intensity ratio <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> goes up from 1.71 to 2.03 (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) with increasing titanium loading in HC-TiN composites and from 1.58 to 2.16 (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) with increasing titanium in HC-TiC. This suggests that the presence of TiN or TiC in the composites increases disorder, in accordance with previous reports of introducing metallic atoms to an amorphous substrate. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66 ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref66 ref67" aria-label="reference 66 and 67">(66,67)</a> There are no obvious strong peaks between 300 and 600 cm<sup>–1</sup>, where the presence of any titanium dioxide would be expected to produce strong peaks (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Raman spectra of HC-TiN (a) and HC-TiC (b) composites with titanium loadings as marked.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS spectra of the HC-TiN and HC-TiC composites are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> and peak attributions from the C 1s, O 1s, N 1s and Ti 2p core-level regions are collected in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>. The measured atomic concentrations of C/N/O/Ti are 83.4:4.7:7.9:4.0 in HC-TiN, while those of C/O/Ti are 92.5:5.1:2.4 in HC-TiC composite, both reflecting surface oxidation of the composites, which were handled in air before loading into the instrument. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref71 ref72 ref73 ref74" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref71 ref72 ref73 ref74" aria-label="reference 71 to 74">(71−74)</a> The survey spectra of both composites and XPS data for the nonloaded HC samples can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8 and S9</a>. The C 1s deconvolution spectra show binding energies of 284.5 and 284.6 eV, respectively, which are typical of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> The peaks with higher binding energy can be attributed to sp<sup>3</sup> C, C–O, C═O, and π–π* shakeup satellites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref68" aria-label="reference 44 and 68">(44,68)</a> The high peak intensity ratio of sp<sup>2</sup>/sp<sup>3</sup> suggests high levels of structural defects in the hard carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref69" aria-label="reference 69">(69)</a> corroborating the above Raman discussion. The obvious peak at lower binding energy of 281.8 eV in HC-TiC is associated with TiC and the smaller shoulder at 283.0 eV corresponds to titanium subcarbide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref70" aria-label="reference 70">(70)</a> The Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> feature at 455.1 eV in HC-TiC also shows the presence of TiC. The peak at 455.4 eV corresponds to TiN in HC-TiN composite, while the peak at higher binding energy of 456.4 eV can be assigned to Ti–O–N, due to charge transfer from Ti to O that causes the chemical state shift toward to higher binding energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref71 ref72 ref73" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref71 ref72 ref73" aria-label="reference 71 to 73">(71−73)</a> The peaks at 458.5 and 458.7 eV correspond to the surface oxide from HC-TiN and HC-TiC, respectively. In addition to the O 1s peak at 530.1 eV, attributable to surface oxidation of the Ti component of the composites, two peaks with higher binding energy are from absorbed carbonyl groups, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref75" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref75" aria-label="reference 75">(75)</a> which is consistent with the discussion of the C 1s region. The peak at 397.4 eV in the N 1s spectrum indicates stoichiometric TiN. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> The peak appears at a lower binding energy of 396.3 eV corresponds to oxynitride species, which is consistent with the Ti–O–N components in Ti 2p analysis. The components at 398.5 and 401.1 eV can be related to oxygen species and free molecular N<sub>2</sub> on the surface of TiN, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref74" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref74" aria-label="reference 74">(74)</a> The N 1s spectrum in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> shows a small nitrogen component (0.1% atom concentration) in HC-N<sub>2</sub>, and the spectrum fitting is good without the presence of C–N in HC-TiN, suggesting no obvious evidence of nitrogen doping in hard carbon or in HC-TiN composite. Some XPS studies of thin film samples use Ar<sup>+</sup> etching before measurement to remove oxide, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref76 ref77" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref76 ref77" aria-label="reference 76 and 77">(76,77)</a> but this is less effective on complex morphologies as studied here (the XRD and Raman do suggest the oxide is thin). The XPS data confirm the presence of TiN and TiC on the carbon surfaces, consistent with the XRD and Raman analysis.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. High-resolution XPS spectra of the C 1s, O 1s, N 1s, and Ti 2p regions for HC-TiN and HC-TiC composites with 15.15 wt % TiN and 8.65 wt % TiC, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2.
