class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="middle" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">285.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">C–C,&nbsp;sp<sup>3</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="middle" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">286.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">C–O</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="middle" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">288.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">C═O</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="middle" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">290.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">satellite</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left" valign="middle">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms of the HC-TiN and HC-TiC composites are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. All can be classified as type IV with H4 hysteresis, implying that the materials possess mesoporosity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref78" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref78" aria-label="reference 78">(78)</a> A feature common to these hysteresis loops is that complete closure points should appear at nitrogen’s boiling point around <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0.42. However, for some materials containing micropores, low-pressure hysteresis can be extended to the lowest attainable pressures as seen in these isotherms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref79 ref80 ref81" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref79 ref80 ref81" aria-label="reference 79 to 81">(79−81)</a> The pore size distribution (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) shows a range of sizes from below 2 nm to around 14 nm. At low concentrations of TiCl<sub>4</sub>, the specific surface areas (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) of the composites increase when compared with that of pure hard carbon. That may be due to particles forming on the surface or the reaction between the titanium chloride and the cellulose may roughen the surface. Further increases in titanium loading increase the TiN or TiC particle size, and then the surface area does not increase further (HC-TiN) or decreases (HC-TiC). The reduced surface area may also be due to covering or filling of micropores near the surface of the HC.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption profile of HC-TiN (a) and HC-TiC (b), with loadings as shown.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The viability of the composites for sodium-ion battery applications was tested in sodium half cells with a 1 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in 1:1 EC/DEC electrolyte. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows the first cycle of charge–discharge profiles of the pure HC fired in N<sub>2</sub> and HC-TiN composites and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the differential capacity plot, emphasizing that the bulk of the charge is passed around 0.1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. The pure hard carbon has the highest first cycle specific capacity of 291 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is competitive with the capacities of other hard carbons obtained from various biomass sources. For example, Hu <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref82" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref82" aria-label="reference 82">(82)</a> reported leaf-based carbon with a charge capacity of 270 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 40 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Xu <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref83" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref83" aria-label="reference 83">(83)</a> obtained nitrogen-rich hard carbon by the pyrolysis of shrimp skin with a capacity of 276 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Ding <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> synthesized hard carbon from peat moss with capacity of 298 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0008.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. First-cycle charge–discharge profiles of HC-N<sub>2</sub> and HC-TiN compositions as shown (a) and cycling performance of HC-N<sub>2</sub> and the 15.15 wt % HC-TiN composite (b) between 2.0 and 0.005 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The capacities of the HC-TiN composites were all similar to 291 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (281 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.89 wt %; 273.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 8.57 wt %; 271 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10.14 wt %; 283 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 15.15 wt %; 261 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 17.27 wt %). The low first-cycle Coulombic efficiency of pure HC (68.4% efficiency) is mainly caused by the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which is proportional to the surface area of HC exposed to the electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref84" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref84" aria-label="reference 84">(84)</a> The measured BET surface area of 15.15 wt % HC-TiN composite is 73 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which may explain its slightly lower first cycle Coulombic efficiency (66.8%), and utilization of this material might require presodiation. The 15.15 wt % HC-TiN composite continued to have capacities similar to those of HC over the first 20 charge/discharge cycles, but then as the capacity of HC drops the composite exhibits a better capacity retention (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b, with a repeat data set in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). At 50 cycles, the HC-TiN composite had a Coulombic efficiency of 99.5% and reversible capacity of 242 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 85.2% of its initial capacity. The HC had a 50 cycles capacity of 216 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, retaining 74.3% of its initial value. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>) shows that the HC and 15.15 wt % HC-TiN composite have similar charge transfer resistances (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) initially, but after 50 cycles, the HC had become more resistive (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 296 Ω) than the composite (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 105 Ω), suggesting that the highly conductive TiN on the surface plays a role to improve the charge transfer kinetics.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0009.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Nyquist plots of HC and 15.15 wt % HC-TiN composites before and after 50 cycles between 2.0 and 0.005 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ grazing incidence XRD patterns of the HC-TiN composites removed from cells at various stages of cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>) showed Cu from the backing foil and TiN. The TiN particle size decreases from 14.7 to 11.2 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) after the first reduction process. At cycle 10, the particle size does not change in the reduced or oxidized electrode, which may be due to reactions occurring only on the surface of TiN. Refinements of the data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/suppl_file/ic9b00116_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) showed no change in the lattice parameter comparing the reduced vs oxidized materials or comparing the materials after 1 versus 10 cycles (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">3</a>). A previous study showed that titanium nitride can be reduced to the metal in lithium batteries, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref85" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref85" aria-label="reference 85">(85)</a> but its storage mechanism in sodium batteries is not clear. Research in our group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> has shown that in several cases only the surface of metal nitrides reacts, and the small variations seen here suggest that TiN has only reacted in the surface region during cycling versus sodium.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0010.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Ex situ grazing incidence XRD patterns of HC-TiN composite-based electrodes at various stages of cycling when reducing to 5 mV and oxidizing back to 2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. Lattice Parameters of TiN in the Composites before Cycling and after Different Cycling Times</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">&nbsp;</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="3" scope="col" align="center" char=".">TiN</th></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">galvanostatic cycling stage at which sample was removed from cell</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">lattice parameter (Å)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>p</sub> (%)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">before cycling</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.24476(19)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.1</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">after first reduction</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.24215(15)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.8</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">after first oxidation</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.2439(3)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.4</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">after tenth reduction</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.2416(2)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.3</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">after tenth oxidation</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.23701(13)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.5</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Galvanostatic cycling of the HC-TiC composite electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a>a) showed the 8.65 wt % HC-TiC composite electrode to have the best capacity (281 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), again a little lower than the pure HC fired in Ar (285 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). The capacity of the other composites was 264 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in 4.28 wt % HC-TiC and 248 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in 16.27 wt % HC-TiC. Previous studies show that TiC can be used to increase the conductivity of composite electrodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref86 ref87" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref86 ref87" aria-label="reference 86 and 87">(86,87)</a> thus enhancing the capacity. This is not observed here; the TiC composites produce slightly lower capacities and, unlike TiN, do not improve the cycling behavior (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a>b). The larger TiC crystallite size of TiC in HC-TiC composite may reduce its ability to participate in reactions, or the TiC surfaces may be less reactive than the TiN. The Coulombic efficiency of the 8.65 wt % HC-TiC composite was 99.6% after 50 cycles, while the capacity decreased from 281 to 187 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles, a capacity retention of 66.5%.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="12" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/medium/ic-2019-00116q_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0011.jpeg" id="gr12" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. (a) Charge–discharge profiles of HC-Ar and HC-TiC composites electrodes between 2.0 and 0.005 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 50 mA/g; (b) cyclic performance of HC-TiC composites in 50 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116/asset/images/large/ic-2019-00116q_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.9b00116&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The HC-TiN composites prepared by the new methodology presented above have some promise in SIBs and the method is applicable to depositing other metal nitrides or carbides on the carbon surfaces. This could be for batteries, but the literature <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref88" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref88" aria-label="reference 19 and 88">(19,88)</a>also suggests that such carbon–metal nitride/carbide composites could be applied in electrocatalysis and supercapacitors.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Reactions of titanium tetrachloride with cellulose followed by carbothermal nitridation or reduction during firing produce hard carbon with nanocrystalline titanium nitride or carbide decorated over the surfaces. In sodium cells both composites show similar first-cycle capacities to hard carbon, but the titanium nitride composite delivers a better capacity retention (85.2%) after 50 cycles than does hard carbon (74.3%). Moreover, we expect the synthesis method demonstrated here will provide an effective way to obtain composites with other metal nitrides and carbides for sodium cell and other energy applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel non-noble electrocatalyst with an av. particle size of approx. 4 nm was prepd. by heat-treating carbon-supported molybdenum tetraphenylporphyrin in a flow NH3 atmosphere.  The effects of heating time and temp. on its electrocatalytic properties were investigated.  The samples obtained were characterized by X-ray diffraction, transmission electron microscopy, XPS, and EDX.  The results show that the synthesized catalyst has the same compn. as that of MoN.  This carbon-supported catalyst displays a strong activity towards oxygen redn. reaction (ORR) and a methanol-tolerant property.  The catalyzed ORR proceeds by an approx. four-electron redn. pathway from O2 directly to H2O.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2006.11.003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chhina, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Campbell, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kesler, O.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Thermal and Electrochemical Stability of Tungsten Carbide Catalyst Supports</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The thermal and electrochem. stability of WC, with and without a catalyst dispersed on it, were studied to evaluate the suitability of the material as an oxidn.-resistant catalyst support.  Std. techniques currently used to disperse Pt on C could not be used to disperse Pt on WC, so an alternative method was developed and used to disperse Pt on both com. WC and on C for comparison of stability.  Electrochem. testing was performed by applying oxidn. cycles between +0.6 V and +1.8 V to the support-catalyst combinations and monitoring the activity of the supported catalyst over 100 oxidn. cycles.  Comparisons of activity change with cumulative oxidn. cycles were made between C and WC supports with comparable loadings of catalyst by wt., solid vol., and powder vol.  WC is more thermally and electrochem. stable than the currently used C support material, Vulcan XC-72R.  However, further optimization of the particle sizes and dispersion of Pt/WC catalyst/support materials and of comparison stds. between new candidate materials and existing C-based supports are required.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adma.200502471"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Blomgren, G.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Monolayer titanium carbide hollow sphere arrays formed via an atomic layer deposition assisted method and their excellent high-temperature supercapacitor performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhong, Yu; Xia, Xinhui; Zhan, Jiye; Wang, Yadong; Wang, Xiuli; Tu, Jiangping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The directed construction of metal carbide arrays with a precise size and specific morphol. still remains a great challenge.  Herein we report a powerful at. layer deposition (ALD)-assisted template synthesis strategy for the prepn. of monolayer titanium carbide (TiC) hollow sphere arrays on a conductive substrate.  Attractive properties, including a porous hollow structure and high elec. cond., have been achieved with the TiC hollow sphere arrays.  When they are characterized as the electrodes of high-temp. (65 °C) org. supercapacitors, impressive performance including high capacitance, superior long-term cycle life (98% retention after 75 000 cycles) and good high-rate ability are demonstrated.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cu3N was examd. as a candidate anode material for rechargeable Li-ion batteries.  Cu3N electrodes had good cycle life and excellent rate capabilities.  The study of the material's electrochem. reaction mechanism revealed that the electrochem. of Cu3N encompass several parallel processes.  In addn. to a reversible Li/Cu nitride conversion process and the formation/decompn. of an org. layer at the surface of the nanocomposite, many cycles and elevated temp. promote a reversible Li/Cu oxide conversion process.  Although the Li/metal nitride conversion process had poor cycling stability, it constituted a fundamental step in the electrode chem. as it generated highly active Cu nanoparticles which activated the formation of an org. layer and the formation of Cu oxide.  The oxidn. of Cu metal to Cu2+ to form CuO and its redn. contributed to the increase in capacity with cycle no.  However, the max. capacity obtained at a high rate and elevated temp. far exceeded the theor. capacity assocd. with the redn. of pure Cu2+ to Cu.  The formation/decompn. of an electrolyte interface layer, which may become more substantial with cycling, and other reaction processes, such as a Li-Cu alloying reaction, may provide the addnl. capacity during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/jp030530s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Z.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yue, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, Q.-Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Reactions of Lithium with Transition Metal Nitride Electrodes</span>. <i>J. The electrochem. reactions of Li with Co3N and Fe3N thin-film electrodes were studied by galvanostatic discharge and charge, cyclic voltammetry, and in-situ spectroelectrochem. measurements.  These thin-film electrodes exhibited reversible discharge capacities ranging from 324 to 420 mA-h/g.  The structure, morphol., and compn. of the as-deposited, lithiated, and delithiated Co3N and Fe3N thin films were characterized by XRD, XPS, and SEM.  The obsd. diffraction peaks from metallic Co(111) and Fe(110) in the lithiated thin films showed the crystallinity of metallic Co and Fe formed by the Li reaction with Co3N and Fe3N, resp., which was also confirmed by their XPS spectra.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Hierarchical Vesicular Carbon Co-Doped with S, P, N for High-Rate Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou Guoqiang; Hou Hongshuai; Guo Tianxiao; Jiang Yunling; Ji Xiaobo; Zou Guoqiang; Hou Hongshuai; Guo Tianxiao; Jiang Yunling; Ji Xiaobo; Foster Christopher W; Banks Craig E; Zhang Yun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchical nanoscale carbons have received wide interest as electrode materials for energy storage and conversion due to their fast mass transfer processes, outstanding electronic conductivity, and high stability.  Here, heteroatom (S, P, and N) doped hierarchical vesicular carbon (HHVC) materials with a high surface area up to 867.5 m(2) g(-1) are successfully prepared using a surface polymerization of hexachloro-cyclotriphosphazene (HCCP) and 4,4'-sulfonyldiphenol (BPS) on the ZIF-8 polyhedrons.  Significantly, it is the first time to achieve a controllability of the wall thickness for this unique carbon, ranging from 18 to 52 nm.  When utilized as anodes for sodium ion batteries, these novel carbon materials exhibit a high specific capacity of 327.2 mAh g(-1) at 100 mA g(-1) after 100 cycles, which can be attributed to the expanded interlayer distance and enhanced conductivity derived from the doping of heteroatoms.  Importantly, a high capacity of 142.6 mAh g(-1) can be obtained even at a high current density of 5 A g(-1), assigning to fast ion/electronic transmission processes stemming from the unique hierarchical vesicular structure.  This work offers a new route for the fabrication/preparation of multi-heteroatom doped hierarchical vesicular materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.02.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong Yoong

                        cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrospun carbon nanofibers and their hybrid composites as advanced materials for energy conversion and storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Shengjie; Li, Linlin; Lee, Jeremy Kong Yoong; Tian, Lingling; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Adams, Stefan; Ramakrishna, Seeram</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">361-395</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Among the various kinds of electrospun materials, electrospun carbon nanofibers (ECNFs) represent one of the most interesting research directions in nanoscience and nanotechnol., with the capability of realizing structural and functional features through tailored designs and controlled synthesis.  The degree of precision in controlling the structures, morphologies, and compns. of ECNFs, together with the incorporation of other materials, allows for the creation of nanoscale building blocks with unique electronic, catalytic, and adsorption properties, thus enabling a variety of promising opportunities in the energy-related field.  This review is centered on the recent major advances in the controlled synthesis of ECNFs, and the potential of the resulting hybrid composites for energy applications.  The promising energy conversion applications of ECNFs and their hybrid composites, such as in solar cells and fuel cells, are also discussed.  The use of ECNFs and their hybrid composites in energy storage technologies such as batteries, supercapacitors, and hydrogen storage is then reviewed.  In each case, we highlight the benefits of materials with high surface area, porous structures, and the free-standing architecture of ECNFs which act to amplify the surface activity and support other materials, leading to enhanced energy conversion and storage performance.  Moreover, we provide a commentary on the challenges, as well as perspectives for potential future research on ECNFs and their hybrid materials.  This review provides an overall perspective on the current state of efforts aimed at the controlled synthesis of ECNF-based materials for their various potential applications, along with the challenges remaining to be addressed, giving an insight into a promising strategy to ameliorate some of the energy issues impacting the world today.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cm401794r"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jun, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, W.-r.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gerbec, J.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrospun Co-TiC nanoparticles embedded on carbon nanofibers: Active and chemically stable counter electrode for methanol fuel cells and dye-sensitized solar cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yousef, Ayman; Brooks, Robert M.; El-Newehy, Mohamed H.; Al-Deyab, Salem S.; Kim, Hak Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">International Journal of Hydrogen Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cobalt-titanium carbide nanoparticles (Co-TiC NPs) embedded on carbon nanofibers (composite) were prepd. by electrospinning of a soln. contg. cobalt acetate tetrahydrate (CoAc), titanium (IV) isopropoxide (TIIP) and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) in acetic acid and ethanol.  It was then subjected to a carbonation process at a low temp. (850 °C) since the composite contains metal carbide.  The obtained composite, as an efficient electrode, was used as an alternative to Pt-free counter electrode (CE) for fuel cells (FCs) and dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs).  Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and chronoamperatory (CA) tests were used to measure the composite electrode's performance in methanol oxidn.  The results showed that the introduced composite could enhance both methanol electro-oxidn. and electrochem. stability as the low onset potential and high c.d. of the composite electrode were obtained at 189 mV and ∼90 mA cm-2 vs.  Ag/AgCl, resp.  The composite also was examd. in dye-sensitized solar cells as counter electrode (CE).  The results showed that the composite electrode was effective, providing stable electrocatalytic activity (ECA) and cond., indicating the composite can improve catalytic activity in triiodide redn.  The short-circuit c.d. (Jsc), open circuit voltage (VOC), fill factor (FF), and energy conversion efficiency (η) were found to be ∼9.98 mA cm-2, 0.758 V, 0.507 and 3.87%, resp.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancing Electrocatalytic Activity for Hydrogen Evolution by Strongly Coupled Molybdenum Nitride@Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Porous Nano-Octahedrons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yanping; Chen, Gao; Xu, Xiaomin; Yang, Guangming; Liu, Meilin; Shao, Zongping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Catalysis</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing highly efficient and affordable noble-metal-free catalysts toward the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) is an important step toward the economical prodn. of hydrogen.  As a nonprecious-metal catalyst for the HER, molybdenum nitride (MoN) has excellent corrosion resistance and high elec. cond., but its catalytic activity is still inadequate.  Here we report our findings in dramatically enhancing the HER activity of MoN by creating porous MoN@nitrogen-doped carbon (MoN-NC) nano-octahedrons derived from metal-org. frameworks (MOFs).  The composite catalyst displays remarkably high catalytic activity, demonstrating a low overpotential of 62 mV at a c.d. of 10 mA cm-2 (η10), a small Tafel slope of 54 mV dec-1, and a large exchange c.d. of 0.778 mA cm-2 while maintaining good stability.  The enhancement in catalytic properties is attributed to the unique nanostructure of the MoN, the high porosity of the electrode, and the synergistic effect between the MoN and the nitrogen-doped carbon substrate.  The performances are among the best ever reported for nonprecious-metal-based electrocatalysts (comparable to those of a 20% Pt/C com. catalyst), making the porous MoN-NC nano-octahedrons some of the most active and acid-stable electrocatalysts for the HER.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA11142H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hector, A.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A mild route to mesoporous Mo2C-C hybrid nanospheres for high performance lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gao, Qing; Zhao, Xinyu; Xiao, Ying; Zhao, Di; Cao, Minhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we have developed a mild route to fabricate typically mesoporous Mo2C-C hybrid nanospheres based on a solvothermal synthesis and redn.-carbonization process.  This work opens a low-temp. route to synthesize valuable carbides.  The resultant Mo2C-C hybrid, for the first time, is used as an anode material in lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  Compared with bulk Mo2C, the Mo2C-C hybrid exhibits much better electrochem. performance.  Remarkably, the hybrid electrode can deliver a specific capacity of over 670 mA h g-1 after 50 cycles at 100 mA g-1, which is much higher than that of the bulk material (113 mA h g-1).  Even cycled at a high c.d. of 1000 mA g-1, high capacities of around 400-470 mA h g-1 can still be retained for the Mo2C-C hybrid.  It might benefit from the synergistic effect of the nanohybridization, effectively relieving the vol. change during the repeated lithium insertion-extn. reactions and maintaining the integrity of the elec. connections.  It is expected that the present synthesis strategy for the Mo2C-C hybrid can be extended to other nanostructured carbides with good energy storage performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1016/0022-4596(79)90191-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nagelberg, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span>. <i>Acc.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32"><span> <i>Web of Science

                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Use of Graphite as a Highly Reversible Electrode with Superior Cycle Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Making Use of Co-Intercalation Phenomena</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jache, Birte; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although being the std. anode material in Li-ion batteries (LIBs), graphite so far is considered to fail application in Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because the Na-C system lacks suitable binary intercalation compds.  This limitation can be circumvented by using co-intercalation phenomena in a diglyme-based electrolyte.  The resulting compd. is a stage-I ternary intercalation compd. with an estd. stoichiometry of Na(diglyme)2C20.  Highlights of the electrode reaction are its high energy efficiency, the small irreversible loss during the 1st cycle, and a superior cycle life with capacities close to 100 mA-h g-1 for 1000 cycles and coulomb efficiencies &gt;99.87%.  A 1-to-one comparison with the analog Li-based cell shows that the Na-based system performs better and also withstands higher currents.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/am507679x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jian, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carter, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vail, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Surface-Area Hard Carbon Anode for Na-Ion Batteries via Graphene Oxide as a Dehydration Agent</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Bommier, Clement; Jian, Zelang; Li, Xin; Carter, Rich; Vail, Sean; Lu, Yuhao; Lee, Jong-Jan; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are emerging as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, particularly for grid-level applications.  Among anode candidate materials, hard carbon is very attractive due to its high capacity and low cost.  However, hard carbon anodes often suffer a low first-cycle coulombic efficiency and fast capacity fading.  In this study, we discover that doping graphene oxide into sucrose, the precursor for hard carbon, can effectively reduce the sp. surface area of hard carbon to as low as 5.4 m2/g.  We further show that such doping can effectively prevent foaming during caramelization of sucrose and extend the pyrolysis burnoff of sucrose caramel over a wider temp. range.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have immense potential as anode materials for Na-ion batteries, because the expanded graphene interlayers and nanovoids between randomly stacked arom. fragments can accommodate a substantial amt. of sodium.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Nanosheet Frameworks Derived from Peat Moss as High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Cui, Kai; Xu, Zhanwei; Zahiri, Beniamin; Tan, Xuehai; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Olsen, Brian C.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11004-11015</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1016/S0378-7753(98)00148-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peled, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Eshkenazi, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rosenberg, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Study of Lithium Insertion in Hard Carbon Made from Cotton Wool</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Study of lithium insertion in hard carbon made from cotton wool</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peled, Emanuel; Eshkenazi, Victor; Rosenberg, Yuri</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1998</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">76</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard-carbon materials were made either by one-step or multi-step pyrolysis of cotton cloth between 700 and 1100°.  All carbons have been characterized by gas sorption, x-ray diffraction and small-angle x-ray scattering techniques.  Two types of carbons have been obtained.  One, made by multi-step pyrolysis, has the highest lithium reversible capacity (∼600 mA-h/g) and two distinct voltage regions: a sloping one between 1.5 and about 0.1 V, called the high-voltage region (HVR), and a horizontal one between 0.1 and 0 V, called the low-voltage plateau (LVP).  The other carbons made by the one-step process have only the HVR and less capacity (≤470 mA-h/g).  The influence of the c.d. and temp. on the capacity and degrdn. rate in both LVP and HVR was checked.  We suggest that there are two different modes of lithium insertion: intercalation-like (on both sides of single graphene sheets) at lower potentials and chem. binding to edge carbon atoms at higher potentials vs. lithium ref. electrode.  A schematic model for lithiated carbon is proposed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889801002242"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Toby, B.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ion-Catalyzed Synthesis of Microporous Hard Carbon Embedded with Expanded Nanographite for Enhanced Lithium/Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Zhi-Long; Xin, Sen; You, Ya; Yu, Le; Lin, Yue; Xu, Da-Wei; Qiao, Chan; Huang, Zhi-Hong; Yang, Ning; Yu, Shu-Hong; Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons attract myriad interest as anode materials for high-energy rechargeable batteries due to their low costs and high theor. capacities; practically, they deliver unsatisfactory performance due to their intrinsically disordered microarchitecture.  Here a facile ion-catalyzed synthesis is reported of a phenol-formaldehyde resin-based hard-carbon aerogel that takes advantage of the chelation effect of phenol and Fe3+, which consists of a three-dimensionally interconnected carbon network embedded with hydrogen-rich, ordered microstructures of expanded nanographites and carbon micropores.  The chelation effect ensures the homodispersion of Fe in the polymer segments of the precursor, so that an effective catalytic conversion from sp3 to sp2 carbon occurs, enabling free rearrangement of graphene sheets into expanded nanographite and carbon micropores.  The structural merits of the carbon offer chances to achieve lithium/sodium storage performance far beyond that possible with the conventional carbon anode materials, including graphite and mesocarbon microbeads, along with fast kinetics and long cycle life.  In this way, the hard carbon proves its feasibility to serve as an advanced anode material for high-energy rechargeable Li/Na batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsuda, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishii, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cumings, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1088/2053-1583/aa814c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Graham, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Development of a stable cation modified graphene oxide membrane for water treatment</span>. <i>2D Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">045006</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Membranes prepd. from layers of graphene oxide (GO) offer substantial advantages over conventional materials for water treatment (e.g. greater flux), but the stability of GO membranes in water has not been achieved until now.  In this study the behavior of GO membranes prepd. with different quantities and species of cations has been investigated to establish the feasibility of their application in water treatment.  A range of cation-modified GO membranes were prepd. and exposed to aq. solns. contg. specific chem. constituents.  In pure water, unmodified and Na-modified GO membranes were highly unstable, while GO membranes modified with multivalent cations were stable provided there were sufficient quantities of cations present; their relative capability to achieve GO stability was as follows: Al3+ Ca2+ Mg2+ Na+.  It is believed that the mechanism of crosslinking, and membrane stability, is via metal-carboxylate chelates and cation-graphite surface interactions (cation-π interaction), and that the latter appears to increase with increasing cation valency.  The instability of cation (Ca or Al)-modified GO membranes by NaCl solns. during permeation occurred as Na+ exchanged with the incorporated multivalent cations, but a high content of Al3+ in the GO membrane impeded Al3+/Na+ exchange and thus retained membrane stability.  In solns. contg. biopolymers representative of surface waters or seawater (protein and polysaccharide solns.), Ca-GO membranes (even with high Ca2+ content) were not stable, while Al-GO membranes were stable if the Al3+ content was sufficiently high; Al-formed membranes also had a greater flux than Ca-GO membranes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47"><span> <i>Inorganic Crystal

Nitrides, such as TiN, VN, CrN, Mn3N2, Fe2N, Co2N and Ni3N were successfully synthesized easily by a simple direct nitriding reaction between metal and fluid N2 in a short time, while any Cu nitrides were not synthesized.                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Absorption correction in precision determination of lattice parameters</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Straumanis, M. E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1959</span>),

    </div><div class="casAbstract">X-ray powder and rotation patterns of W, Si, Cr, TiO, Al, and V were made by using specimens of different thickness; the patterns were measured, the lattice consts. calcd., and plotted against the θ angle or against the Nelson and Riley function (C.A. 39, 47849).  Such curves for thin mounts, semitransparent to x-rays (below 0.2 mm. in diam.), consist of 2 parts: a region of high slope (owing to absorption) and one approaching a zero slope at high Bragg angles.  The latter, varying in extent with the size and nature of the mount, indicates that, owing to the rapid decrease in absorption, the lines in this region are shifted little if any from their correct positions.  Hence, lattice consts. calcd. from the reflections of the high θ angles lying on the horizontal portion of the curve need not be corrected for absorption.  The const., with negligible error, can be calcd. from a single last strong α1 or β reflection.  If an extrapolation is applied, the curve must be traced through points in the high back-reflection region.  Otherwise, any extrapolation, disregarding the horizontal part, would lead to lattice consts. too high in value.  The amt. of line displacement due to absorption in thin samples (below 0.2 mm. in diam). agrees with the geometrical concept of Hadding (Zentr. Mineral. Geol. Pal.ovrddot.aontol. 20, 631(1921)) and Buerger (X-Ray Crystallography 1942, p. 402 (C.A. 36, 47589)).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref60" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1007/BF00624245"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hilti, E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Phases in the Titanium-oxygen System</span>. <i>Naturwissenschaften</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1968</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>55</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">130</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">131</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit63R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">63</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Template-Free One-Pot Synthesis of Porous Binary and Ternary Metal Nitride@N-Doped Carbon Composites from Ionic Liquids</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fechler, Nina; Fellinger, Tim-Patrick; Antonietti, Markus</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">713-719</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein we present a straightforward synthesis approach toward composites of titanium, vanadium, and titanium-vanadium nitride nanoparticles embedded in nitrogen-doped carbon.  These materials can be easily prepd. via the heat treatment of mixts. of the corresponding metal precursors TiCl4 or VOCl3 dissolved in the ionic liq. 1-butyl-3-methyl-pyridinium dicyanamide (Bmp-dca) as the nitrogen/carbon source.  WAXS diffractograms and TEM pictures of the resulting materials reveal the presence of highly cryst. metal nitride nanoparticles with an av. diam. of 5 nm embedded in a graphitic carbon matrix.  XPS measurements show that the carbon network is heavily doped with nitrogen; i.e., it can be described as a nitrogen-doped carbon.  Nitrogen sorption measurements show type I isotherms indicative of mainly microporous composites.  The specific BET surface area increases with increasing amt. of the metal precursor, and it is also dependent on the resp. metal ion used.  Under similar synthetic conditions, the sp. surface areas for VN composites are higher than those of TiN materials, reaching values up to 550 m2.g-1 for VN.  It is worth mentioning that these high surface areas can be reached without a template or etching; thus, it is an inherent structural feature of the composite.  Furthermore, the use of ionic liqs. as precursors offers the possibility for facile processing before material generation; i.e., shaping, printing, or casting can be easily performed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref64"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref64" class="refNumLink">64</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit64" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2005.02.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sadezky, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muckenhuber, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grothe, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niessner, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Poeschl, U.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Microspectroscopy of Soot and Related Carbonaceous Materials. Spectral analysis and structural information</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sadezky, A.; Muckenhuber, H.; Grothe, H.; Niessner, R.; Poeschl, U.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exptl. conditions and math. fitting procedures for the collection and anal. of Raman spectra of soot and related carbonaceous materials were investigated and optimized with a Raman microscope system operated at 3 different laser excitation wavelengths (514, 633, and 780 nm).  Several band combinations for spectral anal. were tested, and a combination of 4 Lorentzian-shaped bands (G, D1, D2, D4) at about 1580, 1350, 1620, and 1200 cm-1, resp., with a Gaussian-shaped band (D3) at ∼1500 cm-1 was best suited for the 1st-order spectra.  The 2nd-order spectra were best fitted with Lorentzian-shaped bands at about 2450, 2700, 2900, and 3100 cm-1.  Spectral parameters (band positions, full widths at half max., and intensity ratios) are reported for several types of industrial C black (Degussa Printex, Cabot Monarch), diesel soot (particulate matter from modern heavy duty vehicle and passenger car engine exhaust, NIST SRM1650), spark-discharge soot (Palas GfG100), and graphite.  Several parameters, in particular the width of the D1 band at ∼1350 cm-1, provide structural information and allow to discriminate the sample materials, but the characterization and distinction of different types of soot is limited by the exptl. reproducibility of the spectra and the statistical uncertainties of curve fitting.  The results are discussed and compared with x-ray diffraction measurements and earlier Raman spectroscopic studies of comparable materials, where different measurement and fitting procedures was applied.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref65" class="refNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit65" data-doi="10.5714/CL.2017.21.051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J. To predict the mixed material formation under these conditions, the precise knowledge of the fundamental mechanisms governing these interactions is essential.  The results of carbon interaction with titanium and silicon, as model substances for metal and covalent carbides, during thermal treatment are presented.                         cas_cit71R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">71</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Substrate-Dependent Reactivity of Water on Metal Carbide Surfaces</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Didziulis, Stephen V.; Frantz, Peter; Perry, Scott S.; El-Bjeirami, Oussama; Imaduddin, Syed; Merrill, Philip B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The interaction of water with two transition metal carbides, titanium carbide (TiC) and vanadium carbide (VC), has been investigated.  The adsorption, reaction, and desorption of water on the (100) face of single-crystal samples of these materials have been studied as a function of substrate temp. over the range 100-600 K.  The adsorption state of water on these surfaces has been probed with high resoln. electron energy loss spectroscopy (HREELS).  The reactivity of water has been directly measured with HREELS and XPS.  The desorption of mol. water and the products of surface reactions has been followed with temp. programmed desorption. Collectively, these measurements indicate that water adsorbs both molecularly and dissociatively on TiC and VC; however, a greater degree of reactivity at cryogenic temps. is obsd. on TiC.  Dissocn. of water produces surface bound hydrogen and hydroxyl groups on both surfaces and a fully dissocd. surface oxide on TiC. Furthermore, a greater participation of the surface carbon atoms is obsd. at the TiC surface through the evolution of COx species at elevated temps.  The differences in surface bonding and desorption profiles are discussed in terms of differences in electronic structure of the two metal carbides.  Some possible implications of these studies for the use of TiC and VC as tribol. materials are also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref72"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref72" class="refNumLink">72</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit72" data-doi="10.1116/1.580764"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Esaka, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Furuya, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shimada, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Imamura, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsubayashi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sato, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishijima, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kawana, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ichimura, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kikuchi, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Comparison of Surface Oxidation of Titanium Nitride and Chromium Nitride Films Studied by x-ray Absorption and Photoelectron Spectroscopy</span>. <i>J.
